| ----- | ------ | ----- | ------ |

<details>
<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK: Translate the following Japanese romaji into natural English. The speaker is a Japanese patient or person talking directly. You are their voice.

RULES:
- Translate LITERALLY and EXACTLY — do not summarise, do not infer diagnosis.
- ALWAYS write in the FIRST PERSON: "I have...", "I feel...", "I can't...", "Hello, I..."
- NEVER use third-person: NEVER write "The patient...", "They report...", "The speaker..."
- Handle ALL language — greetings, everyday phrases, and medical symptoms equally.
- CRITICAL: The input Japanese may be highly fragmented, ungrammatical, or missing particles (e.g. "tsukare ga kanji" => "I feel tired"). Reconstruct the natural meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- Correct romaji misspellings silently (e.g. "zutsu" → headache, "atmaa" → atama = head).
- If unclear, translate conservatively. NEVER invent medical symptoms, medications, or meanings that are not explicitly present in the input. If a word looks like a symptom but doesn't make sense in context, treat it as general speech.
- Output ONLY the English translation — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no known allergies.

Input: zutsuu hidoi
Output: My headache is really bad.

Input: ahsi ga zutsuu de atama mo itai
Output: My leg hurts and my head hurts too.

Input: zutsuu ga hidoi sugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.

Input: konnichiwa genki sou de naniyori desu ne
Output: Hello, you look well, I'm glad to see that.

Input: shinbun wo yondari miru koto shuuchuu dekimasen
Output: I can't concentrate when reading the newspaper or watching things.

Input: hiza wo mageyou to suru toki ni kowabari wo kanjimasu ka
Output: Do you feel stiffness when you try to bend your knee?

Input: hayai dankai de mitsukatta
Output: It was found at an early stage.

NOW TRANSLATE:

MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- term: 無い
  reading: ない / nai
  meaning: nonexistent, not being there, unowned, not had
  type: adj-i, aux-adj
  tags: ichi1, news2, nf45, uk

- term: nai
  reading: nai
  meaning: not
  type: aux-adj, suf
  tags: spec1

SPEECH PATTERN CONTEXT (pragmatic cue — use to capture tone, not as a term to translate):
- pattern: 需要  (じゅよう / juyou)
  nuance: demand, request
Input: wakatta wakatta jaa sono kusuri wo kudasai ima ni mo watashi ni mo kigata no nai desu kara
Output: /no_think
```

</details>

#### LLM Output

> I understand, I understand, so please give me that medicine now, because I don't have time.

TASK: Translate the following Japanese romaji into natural English. The speaker is a Japanese patient or person talking directly. You are their voice.

RULES:
- Translate LITERALLY and EXACTLY — do not summarise, do not infer diagnosis.

- NEVER use third-person: NEVER write "The patient...", "They report...", "The speaker..."

- Correct romaji misspellings silently (e.g. "zutsu" → headache, "atmaa" → atama = head).
- If unclear, translate conservatively. NEVER invent medical symptoms, medications, or meanings that are not explicitly present in the input. If a word looks like a symptom but doesn't make sense in context, treat it as general speech.

<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK: Translate the following Japanese romaji into natural English. The speaker is a Japanese patient or person talking directly. You are their voice.

RULES:
- Translate LITERALLY and EXACTLY — do not summarise, do not infer diagnosis.
- ALWAYS write in the FIRST PERSON: "I have...", "I feel...", "I can't...", "Hello, I..."
- NEVER use third-person: NEVER write "The patient...", "They report...", "The speaker..."
- Handle ALL language — greetings, everyday phrases, and medical symptoms equally.
- CRITICAL: The input Japanese may be highly fragmented, ungrammatical, or missing particles (e.g. "tsukare ga kanji" => "I feel tired"). Reconstruct the natural meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- Correct romaji misspellings silently (e.g. "zutsu" → headache, "atmaa" → atama = head).
- If unclear, translate conservatively. NEVER invent medical symptoms, medications, or meanings that are not explicitly present in the input. If a word looks like a symptom but doesn't make sense in context, treat it as general speech.
- Output ONLY the English translation — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no known allergies.

Input: zutsuu hidoi
Output: My headache is really bad.

Input: ahsi ga zutsuu de atama mo itai
Output: My leg hurts and my head hurts too.

Input: zutsuu ga hidoi sugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.

Input: konnichiwa genki sou de naniyori desu ne
Output: Hello, you look well, I'm glad to see that.

Input: shinbun wo yondari miru koto shuuchuu dekimasen
Output: I can't concentrate when reading the newspaper or watching things.

Input: hiza wo mageyou to suru toki ni kowabari wo kanjimasu ka
Output: Do you feel stiffness when you try to bend your knee?

Input: hayai dankai de mitsukatta
Output: It was found at an early stage.

NOW TRANSLATE:

SPEECH PATTERN CONTEXT (pragmatic cue — use to capture tone, not as a term to translate):
- pattern: 良かったら  (よかったら / yokattara)
  nuance: if you like
Input: hoka no hito ni ugoitari hanshitari no ga sokutei kitdukaremashitaka ochitsukuganaku mata ha sowasowa takusan ogoitemasuka
Output: /no_think
```

<details>
<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK: Translate the following Japanese romaji into natural English. The speaker is a Japanese patient or person talking directly. You are their voice.

RULES:
- Translate LITERALLY and EXACTLY — do not summarise, do not infer diagnosis.
- ALWAYS write in the FIRST PERSON: "I have...", "I feel...", "I can't...", "Hello, I..."
- NEVER use third-person: NEVER write "The patient...", "They report...", "The speaker..."
- Handle ALL language — greetings, everyday phrases, and medical symptoms equally.
- CRITICAL: The input Japanese may be highly fragmented, ungrammatical, or missing particles (e.g. "tsukare ga kanji" => "I feel tired"). Reconstruct the natural meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- Correct romaji misspellings silently (e.g. "zutsu" → headache, "atmaa" → atama = head).
- If unclear, translate conservatively. NEVER invent medical symptoms, medications, or meanings that are not explicitly present in the input. If a word looks like a symptom but doesn't make sense in context, treat it as general speech.
- Output ONLY the English translation — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no known allergies.

Input: zutsuu hidoi
Output: My headache is really bad.

Input: ahsi ga zutsuu de atama mo itai
Output: My leg hurts and my head hurts too.

Input: zutsuu ga hidoi sugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.

Input: konnichiwa genki sou de naniyori desu ne
Output: Hello, you look well, I'm glad to see that.

Input: shinbun wo yondari miru koto shuuchuu dekimasen
Output: I can't concentrate when reading the newspaper or watching things.

Input: hiza wo mageyou to suru toki ni kowabari wo kanjimasu ka
Output: Do you feel stiffness when you try to bend your knee?

Input: hayai dankai de mitsukatta
Output: It was found at an early stage.

NOW TRANSLATE:

SPEECH PATTERN CONTEXT (pragmatic cue — use to capture tone, not as a term to translate):
- pattern: 良かったら  (よかったら / yokattara)
  nuance: if you like
Input: kako ni ninen inai ni guragura ka yakubutsu ya ihou yaku batsu wo shiyou shita mata ha chuushabari koto dearimasu ka ajimasukaku
Output: /no_think
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK: Translate the following Japanese romaji into natural English. The speaker is a Japanese patient or person talking directly. You are their voice.

RULES:
- Translate LITERALLY and EXACTLY — do not summarise, do not infer diagnosis.
- ALWAYS write in the FIRST PERSON: "I have...", "I feel...", "I can't...", "Hello, I..."
- NEVER use third-person: NEVER write "The patient...", "They report...", "The speaker..."
- Handle ALL language — greetings, everyday phrases, and medical symptoms equally.
- CRITICAL: The input Japanese may be highly fragmented, ungrammatical, or missing particles (e.g. "tsukare ga kanji" => "I feel tired"). Reconstruct the natural meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- Correct romaji misspellings silently (e.g. "zutsu" → headache, "atmaa" → atama = head).
- If unclear, translate conservatively. NEVER invent medical symptoms, medications, or meanings that are not explicitly present in the input. If a word looks like a symptom but doesn't make sense in context, treat it as general speech.
- Output ONLY the English translation — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no known allergies.

Input: zutsuu hidoi
Output: My headache is really bad.

Input: ahsi ga zutsuu de atama mo itai
Output: My leg hurts and my head hurts too.

Input: zutsuu ga hidoi sugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.

Input: konnichiwa genki sou de naniyori desu ne
Output: Hello, you look well, I'm glad to see that.

Input: shinbun wo yondari miru koto shuuchuu dekimasen
Output: I can't concentrate when reading the newspaper or watching things.

Input: hiza wo mageyou to suru toki ni kowabari wo kanjimasu ka
Output: Do you feel stiffness when you try to bend your knee?

Input: hayai dankai de mitsukatta
Output: It was found at an early stage.

NOW TRANSLATE:

SPEECH PATTERN CONTEXT (pragmatic cue — use to capture tone, not as a term to translate):
- pattern: deshou  (deshou)
  nuance: it seems, I think, I guess
Input: kensa ga hitsuyou desu ashi no shibire no ga gein kawaranai desu w gein no kekka dattara wo kitsutomerraru deshou
Output: /no_think
```

</details>

#### LLM Output

> I think an examination is necessary because my leg is numb and the numbness doesn't change. If the results of the examination are abnormal, I think I can be sure of it.

TASK: Translate the following Japanese romaji into natural English. The speaker is a Japanese patient or person talking directly. You are their voice.

RULES:
- Translate LITERALLY and EXACTLY — do not summarise, do not infer diagnosis.

- NEVER use third-person: NEVER write "The patient...", "They report...", "The speaker..."

- Correct romaji misspellings silently (e.g. "zutsu" → headache, "atmaa" → atama = head).

<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK: Translate the following Japanese romaji into natural English. The speaker is a Japanese patient or person talking directly. You are their voice.

RULES:
- Translate LITERALLY and EXACTLY — do not summarise, do not infer diagnosis.
- ALWAYS write in the FIRST PERSON: "I have...", "I feel...", "I can't...", "Hello, I..."
- NEVER use third-person: NEVER write "The patient...", "They report...", "The speaker..."
- Handle ALL language — greetings, everyday phrases, and medical symptoms equally.
- CRITICAL: The input Japanese may be highly fragmented, ungrammatical, or missing particles (e.g. "tsukare ga kanji" => "I feel tired"). Reconstruct the natural meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- Correct romaji misspellings silently (e.g. "zutsu" → headache, "atmaa" → atama = head).
- If unclear, translate conservatively. NEVER invent medical symptoms, medications, or meanings that are not explicitly present in the input. If a word looks like a symptom but doesn't make sense in context, treat it as general speech.
- Output ONLY the English translation — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no known allergies.

Input: zutsuu hidoi
Output: My headache is really bad.

Input: ahsi ga zutsuu de atama mo itai
Output: My leg hurts and my head hurts too.

Input: zutsuu ga hidoi sugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.

Input: konnichiwa genki sou de naniyori desu ne
Output: Hello, you look well, I'm glad to see that.

Input: shinbun wo yondari miru koto shuuchuu dekimasen
Output: I can't concentrate when reading the newspaper or watching things.

Input: hiza wo mageyou to suru toki ni kowabari wo kanjimasu ka
Output: Do you feel stiffness when you try to bend your knee?

Input: hayai dankai de mitsukatta
Output: It was found at an early stage.

NOW TRANSLATE:

MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- term: 現在 [grammar, Buddhism]
  reading: げんざい / genzai
  meaning: the present, present time, now, as of
  type: n, adv, adj-no, n-suf, vs, vi
  tags: ichi1, news1, nf01

SPEECH PATTERN CONTEXT (pragmatic cue — use to capture tone, not as a term to translate):
- pattern: kousureba  (kousureba)
  nuance: if you do this, if this is the case
Input: genzai mata kako ni ijikanouserusokudo joutai ni tonattara koto dearimasu ka zensoku mansei heisoku taishikan haien
Output: /no_think
```

<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK: Translate the following Japanese romaji into natural English. The speaker is a Japanese patient or person talking directly. You are their voice.

RULES:
- Translate LITERALLY and EXACTLY — do not summarise, do not infer diagnosis.
- ALWAYS write in the FIRST PERSON: "I have...", "I feel...", "I can't...", "Hello, I..."
- NEVER use third-person: NEVER write "The patient...", "They report...", "The speaker..."
- Handle ALL language — greetings, everyday phrases, and medical symptoms equally.
- CRITICAL: The input Japanese may be highly fragmented, ungrammatical, or missing particles (e.g. "tsukare ga kanji" => "I feel tired"). Reconstruct the natural meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- Correct romaji misspellings silently (e.g. "zutsu" → headache, "atmaa" → atama = head).
- If unclear, translate conservatively. NEVER invent medical symptoms, medications, or meanings that are not explicitly present in the input. If a word looks like a symptom but doesn't make sense in context, treat it as general speech.
- Output ONLY the English translation — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no known allergies.

Input: zutsuu hidoi
Output: My headache is really bad.

Input: ahsi ga zutsuu de atama mo itai
Output: My leg hurts and my head hurts too.

Input: zutsuu ga hidoi sugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.

Input: konnichiwa genki sou de naniyori desu ne
Output: Hello, you look well, I'm glad to see that.

Input: shinbun wo yondari miru koto shuuchuu dekimasen
Output: I can't concentrate when reading the newspaper or watching things.

Input: hiza wo mageyou to suru toki ni kowabari wo kanjimasu ka
Output: Do you feel stiffness when you try to bend your knee?

Input: hayai dankai de mitsukatta
Output: It was found at an early stage.

NOW TRANSLATE:

MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- term: 毎日
  reading: まいにち / mainichi
  meaning: every day, daily
  type: n, adv
  tags: ichi1, news1, nf01

SPEECH PATTERN CONTEXT (pragmatic cue — use to capture tone, not as a term to translate):
- pattern: 飽食  (ほうしょく / houshoku)
  nuance: eating until one is full, eating one's fill, gorging on
Input: hai asa no hozen shoku go mainichi nondemasu uchi gakkoukara ketteiteki gohan wo iwaretayouni tabesasetakara demo saikin jibun amari taberarenakute sugu onaka ga ippai ni natte shimaimsu sukoshi taberu dake de sugu
Output: /no_think
```

</details>

#### LLM Output

> Yes, I eat breakfast every day. Even though I am told to eat a set amount of rice from school, recently I can't eat much, and my stomach gets full immediately. Even if I eat just a little, it gets full right away.

<details>
<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK: Translate the following Japanese romaji into natural English. The speaker is a Japanese patient or person talking directly. You are their voice.

RULES:
- Translate LITERALLY and EXACTLY — do not summarise, do not infer diagnosis.
- ALWAYS write in the FIRST PERSON: "I have...", "I feel...", "I can't...", "Hello, I..."
- NEVER use third-person: NEVER write "The patient...", "They report...", "The speaker..."
- Handle ALL language — greetings, everyday phrases, and medical symptoms equally.
- CRITICAL: The input Japanese may be highly fragmented, ungrammatical, or missing particles (e.g. "tsukare ga kanji" => "I feel tired"). Reconstruct the natural meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- Correct romaji misspellings silently (e.g. "zutsu" → headache, "atmaa" → atama = head).
- If unclear, translate conservatively. NEVER invent medical symptoms, medications, or meanings that are not explicitly present in the input. If a word looks like a symptom but doesn't make sense in context, treat it as general speech.
- Output ONLY the English translation — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no known allergies.

Input: zutsuu hidoi
Output: My headache is really bad.

Input: ahsi ga zutsuu de atama mo itai
Output: My leg hurts and my head hurts too.

Input: zutsuu ga hidoi sugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.

Input: konnichiwa genki sou de naniyori desu ne
Output: Hello, you look well, I'm glad to see that.

Input: shinbun wo yondari miru koto shuuchuu dekimasen
Output: I can't concentrate when reading the newspaper or watching things.

Input: hiza wo mageyou to suru toki ni kowabari wo kanjimasu ka
Output: Do you feel stiffness when you try to bend your knee?

Input: hayai dankai de mitsukatta
Output: It was found at an early stage.

NOW TRANSLATE:

SPEECH PATTERN CONTEXT (pragmatic cue — use to capture tone, not as a term to translate):
- pattern: 違和感  (いわかん / iwakan)
  nuance: uncomfortable feeling, feeling out of place, sense of discomfort
Input: nnnn hakike hidoi no to shigokan dake desu ato yoko ni natte okiagaru toki ya tatte suwatte tachi agaru toki ni chotto memai yousuruni desu onaka mo katai kanji shite itsumo i ga itai desu senjutsu kuroi ben detanto desu ga nani ka warui koto da to omoidasu ka
Output: /no_think
```

</details>

#### LLM Output

> I have a strange feeling. It's really bad. When I turn over to my side, or when I stand up from sitting, I feel a bit dizzy. My stomach also feels uncomfortable, and I always have pain. I think something is wrong.

#### Tone / Pragmatic Context Hint

| Score | Romaji | Kanji | Nuance |
| ----- | ------ | ----- | ------ |
| 0.6609 | `yoreba` | — | according to |

<details>
<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK: Translate the following Japanese romaji into natural English. The speaker is a Japanese patient or person talking directly. You are their voice.

RULES:
- Translate LITERALLY and EXACTLY — do not summarise, do not infer diagnosis.
- ALWAYS write in the FIRST PERSON: "I have...", "I feel...", "I can't...", "Hello, I..."
- NEVER use third-person: NEVER write "The patient...", "They report...", "The speaker..."
- Handle ALL language — greetings, everyday phrases, and medical symptoms equally.
- CRITICAL: The input Japanese may be highly fragmented, ungrammatical, or missing particles (e.g. "tsukare ga kanji" => "I feel tired"). Reconstruct the natural meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- Correct romaji misspellings silently (e.g. "zutsu" → headache, "atmaa" → atama = head).
- If unclear, translate conservatively. NEVER invent medical symptoms, medications, or meanings that are not explicitly present in the input. If a word looks like a symptom but doesn't make sense in context, treat it as general speech.
- Output ONLY the English translation — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no known allergies.

Input: zutsuu hidoi
Output: My headache is really bad.

Input: ahsi ga zutsuu de atama mo itai
Output: My leg hurts and my head hurts too.

Input: zutsuu ga hidoi sugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.

Input: konnichiwa genki sou de naniyori desu ne
Output: Hello, you look well, I'm glad to see that.

Input: shinbun wo yondari miru koto shuuchuu dekimasen
Output: I can't concentrate when reading the newspaper or watching things.

Input: hiza wo mageyou to suru toki ni kowabari wo kanjimasu ka
Output: Do you feel stiffness when you try to bend your knee?

Input: hayai dankai de mitsukatta
Output: It was found at an early stage.

NOW TRANSLATE:

MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- term: iyou
  reading: iyou
  meaning: hey, hello, hi, wow
  type: int
  tags: none

SPEECH PATTERN CONTEXT (pragmatic cue — use to capture tone, not as a term to translate):
- pattern: yoreba  (yoreba)
  nuance: according to
Input: wakatta sensei kore ijou warukunai uranai to iinokosu desu ga sugi no youyaku itsu desuka saisho chichi no hi de happyoukai aru no de
Output: /no_think
```

</details>

#### LLM Output

> I understand, teacher. I don't think it's worse than this, but I'm not sure when exactly it is. There's a celebration on Father's Day, so...
